TO THE PSALM








A KEY TO THE PSALMS:
BEING A
TABULAR ARRANGEMENT,
BY WHICH THE PSALMS ARE EXHIBITED TO THE EYE ACCORDING TO A
GENERAL RULE OF COMPOSITION PREVAILING IN THE HOLY SCRIPTURES.
BY THE LATE
REV.THOMAS BOYS, M.A.,
OF TRINITY COLLEGE, CAMBRIDGE.
EDITED, WITH INTRODUCTION, NOTES AND AN APPENDIX ON THE STRUCTURE
OF THE PSALMS AS A WHOLE,
BY THE
REV. E. W. BOLLINGER, D.D.
WITH PREFACE AND MEMOIR BY THE REV. SYDNEY THELWALL, B.A.

PREFACE AND MEMOIR.
T HAVE been asked to prefix to this book a brief Memoir of Thomas Boys. Always
premising that I can only do this “ to the best of my knowledge and belief,” I
gladly comply; for the remembrance of those “ who have spoken to us the word of
God,” that, “observing the end of their conversation,” we may “imitate their faith,’"
is a Scriptural injunction.
Thomas Boys, the only son of Admiral Boys, of Ramsgate, was born on the i/th
of June, 1792. Educated at Tunbridge School, he went thence to Trinity College,
Cambridge, where he gained his scholarship in his Freshman’s year, a great thing at
Trinity. However, severe study so affected his health that he gave up the idea of
competing for honours, and came out “Captain of the Poll” —that is, first of all the
men of that year who took the Ordinary Degree —hundx*eds of marks ahead of the
second man. He must have been very young at this time; for in 1813 or 1814 he was
serving under the Iron Duke in the Peninsula. Probably it was thought that, with
an Admiral for his father and a General for his uncle, his “prospects” in the service
were hopeful. But April of the year 1814 saw the battle of Toulouse, and the end of
that awful war of which Hapier has given us so graphic a history. Thus Boys had
little opportunity of winning distinction. Distinction he had won, though, already ;
for, lad as he was, he had volunteered to conduct some treasure very near, if not
through, the enemy’s lines, and succeeded in doing it; and a sabre-cut above the eye
was another distinction, of a practical and honourable (if painful) kind. Had this been
all, the book to which this forms the preface might never have been written; or it
might have been some one else’s privilege to find that key of David which unlocks so
many treasures of his cabinet. But, in 1841, there appeared a volume called “ The
Protestant Annual”; and in that volume the gem is a short tale, of thrilling interest,
entitled “ The Confessional: by an Old Campaigner.” I could wish to reproduce that
tale here ; for to abridge it would be to spoil it. It must suffice to say thus much about
it: that it relates certain incidents, connected with the Peninsular War, in which the
writer, a Jew, “Isaac,” and his wife, “Rebecca,” and a young Portuguese officer,
Luis da M , are the central figures; that it is “ a tale of mystery and of treachery ;
and that it contains, as I believe, the real history of Mr. Boys’s conversion “ from dark-
ness to light, and from the power of Satan unto God. ” The battle of Toulouse, then,


not only ended, for him, that particular war, but his military career altogether He
enlisted under a new Captain. The weapon of steel was exchanged for “ the sword of
the Spirit.” The gallant young lieutenant became a gallant “officer of Jesus Christ.”
And so deeply did he feel the superiority of his later over his earlier service, that
when, at the Duke of Wellington’s funeral in 1852, he, as one of the surviving veterans,
was awarded a place of honour in the Cathedral, he would not consent to go till he had
ascertained that, being a Clergyman, he would be excused from wearing “ a mourning
sword.”
Whether it were tlxe incidents connected with “Isaac and Rebecca” which kindled
his sympathy for “Israel after tlie flesh,” and his zeal for the Hebrew tongue, or no,
certain it is that he became so expert a Hebraist that the palm of Hebrew scholarship
in England was, as years went on, said to lie between him, Tregelles (a pupil of Boys’s,
I fancy) and McCaul; and that he not only became officially connected with the
Society for Promoting Christianity among the Jews, particularly with their Educational
Institution in Palestine Place, and editor of their “Expositor,” but maintained his
interest in that wonderful People to the last. With the Jews’ Society was connected
another scholar of Trinity College, Cambridge,—my father, the Rev A. S. Thelwall.
He, throwing up all Cambridge prospects, had gone, as a Deacon, ordained to this
special post, in 1819, at Simeon’s urgent request, to undertake the double duty of
Chaplain to the English residents and Missionary to the Jews, at Amsterdam,
Between him (the first clergyman to be thus a missionary to the Jews) and Boys
there soon sprang up a friendship which, cemented by common sympathies and com-
mon persecutions, never ceased. To those two men, reared, as so many of our Reformers
were, at Cambridge (which, says even the worldly Whig Macaulay, “ had the honour
of educating the Bishops whom Oxford had the honour of burning”), was granted by
God the distinction of detecting the insidious beginnings of that neology which is now
(as Boys wrote to me long years afterwards) “established in the land”; and of
opposing it, and, along with it, Romanism, especially in its Puseyite developments, with
all their power. And here let me record my sad and deep suspicion that the treatment
accorded to these “two witnesses” and their scanty band of comrades, accorded, too,
by the Evangelical party itself as well as by others, goes very far toward accounting for
the state of that party, and the state of the Church of England at large, nor, perhaps,
of the Church of England only, at this time. Their fidelity to their principles was
shown, for instance, by their attachment to the Trinitarian Bible Society; in connection
with which Boys (as Editorial Superintendent) spent two years at Lisbon, revising the
Portuguese translation of the Bible ; and of which my father was a well-known secretary;
and by their vigorous- championing of “ sound doctrine from the pulpit, on the
and in the press. Yet so little was either of them appreciated that the one could say he


bad not received money enough from Church preferment to pay his college expenses,
moderate as they were; and Boys never had a church entrusted to him till his old
friend Babbage, the famous mathematician, took a small pamphlet which he had pub-
lished—l think on some astronomical topic —to Blomfield, who was then Bishop of
London, and said;—“ My Lord, it is a disgrace to us that the man who could write
such a pamphlet as that should be without preferment among us.” And how did the
Bishop act P He appointed him, now no longer in the prime and vigour of life, to a
new Church, one of the so-called Peel Churches, in Wenlock Road, Hoxton, for which
he was quite unsuited. A most inconsiderate appointment it was; for such a district
as that wanted, not a profound scholar, long and sorely tried by domestic sorrows and
systematic neglect and nnkindness, but a young man in the full buoyance of health
and energy, like the Apostles when they entered on their work. And that was the
only “ Preferment ”he ever held. Retiring from the active duties of it when
threatened with paralysis in the ptilpit, he continued nominally Incumbent till
his death. But how many in that district knew their privilege in having such a man
as its first Vicar, or their danger in opposing and vexing him, is a question which may
be suggested, but cannot be answered, in these pages. Various parishes he had served,
at different times, as Curate, or as Assistant:—such as Crayford and North Cray, in
Kent; Widford, in Hertfordshire; High Wycombe, Bucks, when Charles Bradley was
in charge there; St. Dunstan’s-in-the-West, Fleet Street; St. Mark’s, Myddelton
Squai’e, where the present Lord Chancellor will probably remember him well. But
this was his sole Incumbency. Perhaps, indeed, the place where for years he became
best known - and best appreciated - was the Reading-room of the British Museum.
There, his tall form and capacious head were daily conspicuous; and, while his ample
stores of knowledge were to some extent worthily admired, his stately courteousness,
keen humour, and grave power of checking any unseemly speech, found acknowledg-
ment even where the capacity of appreciating his deep spirituality was lacking.
To enumerate all his literary efforts is quite beyond my power. Indeed,
I suppose there is no man living who could enumerate them; because so much was
done anonymously. But the two works most intimately related to this present
volume were his Tactica Sacra, published in 1824, and his Key to the Booh of Psalms
(which has suggested the title of the present work), published in 1825. From
1827 —1830 he edited that masterly periodical, The Christian Review and Clerical
Magazine. What led to his discovery (or re-discovery) of the great principle of
Parallelism, or (as he preferred to call it) Correspondence, I know not. But a
little consideration of the many illustrations of that principle with which our own
bodies furnish us will of itself go far to show us how true to nature the principle is;
and whenever we get hold of anything that is true to nature as God made it, not as


sin lias deformed it, we may he pretty sure we are on a right track. Others, no
doubt, as he readily admits, had done more or less in the same path before him.
But, to my mind, he is by far the greatest master of the subject (as, indeed, he is one
of the most suggestive commentators on Holy Scripture, ancient or modern) whom
I know. As he “went up higher” on the 2nd of September, xBBo, in his 89th year,
this, it will be seen, is a posthumous giving to the Church of Christ some of the fruits
of his labours. But Dr. Bullinger, into whose hands the editorship has fallen, and
who, in fact, suggested the publication, has entered so fully into the subject, and is
so well qualified to deal with it, that it seems as if he had been specially chosen of
God to the task. That he should be Secretary of that Bible Society in connection
with which Mr. Boys, as we have seen, revised (or re-made) the Portuguese trans-
lation of the Scriptures, and that he should have been the first to affix to this trans-
lation marginal references, are coincidences not unworthy of remark by such as love
to trace “the finger of God” in the occurrences of daily life. Dom Pedro, if I mistake
not, publicly, in the market-place of Lisbon, thanked Mr. Boys for his version, and
said, while shaking hands with him, “What will my Catholic subjects think of my
shaking hands with a heretic P” What reception will be awarded to this “ Key ”by
Protestant scholars and students remains to be seen.
SYDNEY THELWALL.
Westleigh Vicarage,
North Devon.


INTRODUCTION.
r PHE works of the Lord are perfect; and the greatest of His works is His Word.
1 We are not of those who put these in opposite camps, as though they were
antagonistic the one to the other; or as though it were our great business to reconcile
the Word of God with His Works; hut we put God’s Word at the head of His
Works as the greatest and most important of them all. If we understand the Word,
it is comparatively of little importance whether we understand the Works ; for it
is the Word and not the Works which is “able to make us wise unto salvation.”
(2 Tim. iii. 15.) There cannot possibly be any contradiction between them, seeing
that they both proceed from the same Author.
“The Works of the Lord are great, sought out of all them that have pleasure
therein.” The Microscope and the Telescope are both necessary; and, while they
have both been employed in seeking out the Works, they have not been equally
employed in searching out the Divine Word. For the most part the Microscope is
used; and single words, verses, and isolated passages are buried in a vast accumulation
of the words, notes and thoughts of commentators; while the Telescope is but little
used; and Students read on, forgetful and unmindful of the fact that each line and
each verse are only members of some larger structure, and that these larger structures
are only parts of others that go to make up a perfect whole. The Word thus corres-
ponds, in the perfection of its structure, to all the other Works of God.
Jehovah has been pleased to reveal Himself to man: and He has employed not
the words of angels, but human words, words pertaining to the Earth ; but they are
“ pure words,” and they are perfectly constructed. Sometimes they are put into
peculiar forms (or “figures”) for the purpose of special emphasis; but they are
always pure and perfect.
Many of the phenomena connected with their structure have been observed from
the earliest times, but it was De Rossi,* a most learned Jew of the 16th century, who
first published a vast mass of ancient Jewish learning bearing on the Sacred Text, in
his remarkable work, Meor Enajim {i.e., The Light of the Eyes). Bishop Lowth has
given an English translation of chapter lx.,f which deals with the construction of
words and lines. He also developed the doctrine of what he called Parallelisms , and
showed how in two or more lines word answers to word. These he divided into three
classes :
(1) Synonymous or gradational, e.g.,
'■ My soul doth magnify the Lord :
My spirit hath rejoiced in God my Saviour/’ (Luke i. 46, 47-)
Here, “spirit” answers to “soul,” “rejoice” answers to “magnify,” and “the Lord
answers to “God my Saviour. ’
* See article Rossi, Kitto's Biblical Cyclopedia, vol. iii. p. 702.
+ See Lowth’s Translation of Isaiah, Preliminary Dissertation, p. xxviii. (15th Ed., 1857).


(2) Antithetic (or opposite), e.g.,
‘ 1 Faithful are the wounds of a friend;
But deceitful are the kisses of an enemy.” (Prov. xxvii. 6.)
Here we have “deceitful” as the opposite of “faithful,” “kisses” as the opposite of
“wounds,” “enemy” as the opposite of “friend.”
(3) Synthetic, or Constructive , where the parallelism consists only in the
similar form of construction, e.g.,
“ The law of the Lord is perfect, converting the soul:
The testimony of the Lord is sure, making wise the simple.
The statutes of the Lord are right, rejoicing the heart:
The commandment of the Lord is pure, enlightening the eyes.
The fear of the Lord is clean, enduring for ever:
The judgments of the Lord are true and righteous altogether. (Ps. xix. 7 —9.)
Bishop Jebb (in his Sacred Literature ) carried this principle further. He extended
the doctrine of Parallelism with reference to the Old Testament, and applied its
principles to the Hew, grouping a number of lines into paragraphs or stanzas. This
led to the discovery of a fourth class of parallelisms, which could not be seen until a
number of lines were thus brought together. He called this “ Introversion ;” where, with
any number of lines the first answers to the last, the second to the penultimate, and so
throughout, till we come to the two corresponding lines in the middle.
It was reserved for Thomas Boys, who was indeed indebted for some of his first
lights to former writers, really to develope and apply the principles which they had
formulated. Those principles, which had heretofore been applied only to short passages,
he applied to long ones; and he arranged whole chapters and books, as Bishops Lowth
and Jebb had arranged verses. He discarded the word Parallelism as altogether
inadequate; and preferred the word u Correspondence as more satisfactory, combining
and describing all the phenomena. “ Poetry,” also, as it had hitherto been called, was
found to be inadequate; inasmuch as precisely the same phenomena, the same perfection
of structure, and the same orderly arrangement are found in Genesis, the Historical
Books, the Gospels and the Epistles; in Old and New Testaments alike.
It will readily be granted that the sacred writers must have had a method of some
kind. It is difficult to imagine, and absurd to suppose, that the arrangements which we
discover are purely the result of accident. Every human writer has some order and
method; we cannot deny this, therefore, to the holy men of old. The Holy Ghost, by
Whom they were moved, has displayed order and arrangement in all the other works
of God: why should we not expect to find the same in the Word which He inspired ?
Whether the arrangement and design existed in the mind of the human instrument or
only in the mind of the Spirit, Who dictated, cannot be determined. One thing we do
know; and that is that the sacred penmen were not always fully aware of the purport of
that which they wrote (1 Peter i. 10, 11). It is equally possible, therefore, that the
words and their order were not understood by them to their full extent. Indeed, in
some Scriptures we discover an arrangement so marvellous and complicated that it
could hardly have been present, in all its parts, to the mind of the sacred writer at the
moment of writing it. It may be, also, that, as in the other works of God, there might
be latent perfections, to be discovered in after ages for the confirmation of the faith of
His People, and of the Truth of His Wbrd.
If this be so, then the duty and advantage of studying this subject do not need
enforcement. It must subserve a useful purpose, and we cannot ignore or neglect it


without loss to ourselves. It is by no means merely a matter of criticism or curious
disquisition, but a sacred thing. It will he the duty of everyone who aspires to
undeistand the Whrd of God, to procure all obtainable information respecting it.
And here it ought to be stated, that, to pursue the study of this subject thoroughly,
a knowledge of the original languages is indispensable. Ho translation transfers, or can
transfer, all the arrangements and correspondences. Indeed, from the fact that the
translators did not themselves perceive them, it is impossible they should preserve them;
while, on the other hand, undesigned and accidental correspondences may be discovered
in a translation, which in the Original have nothing that answers to them, and are
therefore without Divine authority. In some of the shorter and verbal arrangements
no rendering can accurately reproduce the symmetry of the original in another
language. But still there is much, very much, that an English Bible student may
accomplish; more perhaps than he will ever have time to discover or power to digest.
Great will be the reward of all who are led to devote any of their energies to this
study. Even ■when they fail to make a desired discovery, search for it will not be
fruitless; for the very act of searching for it will be a new reading of the Scriptures,
full of new delights.
The “arrangements” are almost invariably connected with the sense of the
passages, and this sense is generally unimpaired in translation. Where the corres-
pondence is of a more delicate and less obvious kind, there the discovery is often of
the greatest value in helping us to the true meaning of the passage. The corres-
pondence may not be at once seen, because the meaning of one or both passages taken
separately is not understood. But, when they are taken together, and placed one over
against the other, each becomes clear from the other’s light.
All the arrangements come under one of three forms ; but, as the third is only a
combination of the other two, these are really reduced to two fundamental forms:
(0 Alternate; (2) Introverted. We will give a few examples of each; merely
premising that the use of certain letters and signs is purely arbitrary, and for the
sake of convenience. Corresponding members are marked by the same letter; one in
Roman type (A), the other in italic type (A). These must be read together and com-
pared. Where there are more than two members, the letters are numbered (A 1 , A 2,
&c.), or corresponding Greek letters are employed. Until any can show a better
method, we must continue to use those employed by Thomas Boys. We ought,
perhaps, to note further here, by way of explanation, which will serve for the whole
book, that the first part of a verse is indicated thus 1-, the last part thus -1, and, if it
is necessary still further to divide it, a middle or inner part is indicated thus, -1-.
The words which mark the correspondence we print in darker type.
(i.) Alternate Arrangements.
a | The vile person shall no more be called liberal,
b | nor the churl said to be bountiful.
a For the vile person will speak villany, and his heart will work iniquity, to practise
hypocrisy, and to utter error against the Lord, to make empty the soul of the
hungry, and he will cause the drink of the thirsty to fail.
b I The instruments also of the churl are evil: he deviseth wicked devices to destroy
| the poor with lying words, even when the needy speaketh right. (Is. xxxii, 5 —7.)
In a. and a., the vile person ; a., negative, and a., positive. In b. and h., the churl;
b., negative, and 6,, positive.


a | A new heart also will I give you,
b 1 and a new spirit will I put within you ;
a I and I will take away the stony heart out of your flesh, and I will give you an
1 heart of flesh.
b 1 And 1 will put my spirit within you. (Ezeb. xxxvi. 26, 27.)
In a. and a., the promise of a new heart; in b. and 6., the promise of a new spirit.
a 1 Cursed is the man that trusteth in man, &e.
b | For he shall he like the heath in the desert, &c.
a 1 Blessed is the man that trusteth in the Lord, &c.
b 1 For he shall he as a tree planted by the waters, &c. (Jer. xvii. 5—8.)
In a. and a., we have trust; in b. and b., comparison.
a 1 And he overthrew those cities,
b 1 and all the plain,
a | and all the inhabitants of the cities,
b | and that which grew upon the ground. (Gen. xix. 25.)
Here we have, in a. and a., the cities and their inhabitants ; while in b. and b., we
have the plain and its produce.
a 1 Grant me the place of this threshingfloor ;
b | and I will build an altar therein unto the Lord :
a 1 thou shalt grant it me for the full price :
b 1 and the plague shall be stayed from the people. (1 Chron. xxi, 22.)
Here we have, in a. and a ., David’s request; while in b. and b., we have his
design.
It will be observed in all these and other instances that, a., being in correspondence
with a., b. is practically in a parenthesis, and we may read on from a. to a., without any
break in the sense. So with b. and b., which maybe read in connection with each other,
a., being practically in a parenthesis.
We have a beautiful example of this in Hebrews i. and ii. These two chapters are
arranged in an alternate structure of four members, thus :
a | Heb. i. t, 2-.
b | -2—14.
a 1 Heb. ii. I—4.1 —4.
6 | s—lB.
Here we have —
a | God speaking.
b 1 The Sou—God—“better than the angels.”
a | God speaking.
b 1 The Son—Man—“lower than the angels.’’
How, it will be observed that a. begins with “ therefore ” (ii. 1) ; but this “ there-
fore ”is consequent, not on the end of b. (i. 14), but on the end of a. (i. 2-). If “ God
who at sundry times and in divers manners spake in time past unto the fathers by the
prophets, hath in these last days spoken unto us by His Son. . . . Therefore we ought
to give the more earnest heed to the things which we have heard, lest, &c.” So also
the “ for” with which b. begins (ii. 5) is consequent, not on the end of a. (ii. 4), but on
the end of b. (i. 14) : —“Are they not all ministering spirits, sent forth to minister for
them who shall be heirs of salvation ? . . . . For unto the angels hath he not put in
subjection the world to come, &c.” Thus, the intervening member is practically in a
parenthesis.


Again:—
a | I have planted,
b | Apollos watered,
c | but God gave the increase.
a | So then neither is he that planteth any thing,
b | neither he that watereth ;
c | but God that giveth the increase. (i Cor. iii. 6,7.)
(2) Introverted Arrangements.
a | Make the heart of this people fat,
b | and make their ears heavy,
c 1 and shut their eyes ;
c 1 lest they see with their eyes,
h | and hear with their ears,
a [ and understand with their heart. (Is. vi. 10.)
Here, in the extreme members, a. and a., we have the heart; in b. and h., the ears;
and in c. and c., the eyes.
a | Follow not that which is evil,
b 1 but that which is good.
b | He that doeth good is of God :
a | He that doeth evil hath not seen God. (3 John 11.)
Here, in the extremes a. and a., we have evil, and negatives j while in the central
members, b. and 6., we have good, and positives.
a | Holding faith,
b | and a good conscience;
b 1 which some having put away,
a | concerning the faith have made shipwreck. (1 Tim. i. 19.)
Here we learn from the correspondences that, it is a good conscience which is put
away, and that the shipwreck is concerning the faith.
a | They saw that the gospel of the nucireumcision was committed unto me,
b 1 as the gospel of the circumcision was unto Peter:
b j for he that wrought effectually in Peter to the Apostleship of the circumcision,
a | the same was mighty in me toward the Gentiles. (Gal. ii. 7,8.)
In a. and a., we have St. Paul’s commission to the Gentiles; in b. and b., St. Peter’s
commission to the Jews.
a | Blessed be God,
b j even the Father of our Lord Jesus Christ;
b | the Father of mercies,
a | and the God of all comfort. (2 Cor. i. 3.)
In a. and a., Deity; in b. and h., Paternity,
a | If God spared not the natural branches,
b | take heed lest He also spare not thee,
c 1 Behold therefore the goodness
d ] and severity of God:
d | on them which fell, severity ;
c | but toward thee, goodness, if thou continue in His goodness;
b | otherwise, thou also shalt be cut off.
a I And they also, if they abide not still in unbelief, shall be graffed in : for God is
I able to graff them in again. (Rom. xi. 21 23.)
Here we may express the Correspondence by marking the descriptive terms in the
form of a “ Skeleton,” according to the plan adopted throughout the Psalms :
a | The natural branches,
b | “ thee.”
c | goodness
d | severity.
d | severity,
c | goodness.
• b | “ thee.”
a j The natural branches.


Again a I And now also the axe is laid unto the root of the trees ; therefore every tree
I which bringeth not forth good fruit is hewn down, and cast into the Are.
b | I indeed baptize you with water unto repentance;
c | but lie that cometh after me is mightier than I,
c | whose shoes I am not worthy to bear.
h 1 He shall baptize you with the Holy Ghost and with fire ;
a I whose fan is in his hand, and he will throughly purge his floor, and gather his
1 wheat into his garner; but he will bum up the chad: with unquenchable lire ’
(Matt. iii. 10 —12.)
The following “ Skeleton” will describe the correspondences :
a | "Warning,
b j Baptism.
c 1 Christ and John.
c ( Christ and John.
b | Baptism.
a | Warning,
It will be observed that in a. and a., the Lord is referred to : in a., as a hewer of trees;
in a., as a winnower of corn; in a., we have the axe of the hewer; and in a., the fan of the
winnower. In a., we have the distinction between good and bad trees ; in a., the distinction
between wheat and chaff. In both a. and a., we have the imminence of the danger
from the same avenging fire. With all these contrasts, there is the one correspondence
of “ warning.”
In c. and c., we have the superiority of Christ to John.
In b. and &., the superiority of our Lord’s baptism to John’s.
a | We know that thou art true
b j and carest for no man,
h | for thou regardest not the person of men,
a | but teachest the way of God in truth. (Mark xii. 14.)
In a, and a., we have truth; b. and ?>., independence, or impartiality.
a | Jesus saith unto him, Rise, take up thy bed, and walk,
b | And immediately the man was made whole,
c J and took up his bed, and walked;
d | and on the same day was the sabbath.
d | The Jews therefore said unto him that was cured, It is the sabbath day:
c | it is not lawful for thee to carry thy bed.
b 1 He answered them, He that made me whole,
a j the same said unto me, Take up thy bed, and walk. (John v. B—n.)
In d. and d., we have the sabbath; in c. and c., the bed carried; in b. and b., the
man made whole; in a. and a., the words of our Lord.
a | And he gave him none inheritance in it, no, not so much as to set his foot on:
b 1 yet he promised that he would give it to him for a possession,
b | and to his seed after him,
a j when as yet he had no child. (Acts vii. 5.)
In a. and a., we have the double obstacle; in b. and h ., the double promise.
a And the Lord God called unto Adam, and said unto him, Where art thou ? And he
said, I heard thy voice in the garden, and I was afraid, because I was naked ; and I
hid myself. And he said, Who told thee that thou wast naked ? Hast thou eaten of
the tree, whereof I commanded thee that thou shouldest not eat ? And the man said,
b The woman whom thou gavest to be with me, she gave me of the tree, and I did eat.
And the Lord God said unto the woman, What is this that thou hast done ? and the
woman said,
c | The serpent beguiled me, and I did eat.
c ] And the Lord God said unto the serpent, Because thou hast done this, &c.,
b | Unto the woman he said, 1 will greatly multiply thy sorrow and thy conception; &c.

Sometimes these arrangements are of considerable service to us in the explanation
of difficult passages, and in the unravelling of such as are complicated. Notice a few
examples: —
a | The incense that ye burned in the cities of Judah, and in the streets of Jerusalem,

Here we at once perceive that the “ them” in b., refers to b., viz., to “ your fathers,
&c. So also the “it ”of a., refers, not to the idolatry of b., but to the “incense of a.

Here Balak is the subject of a., and Balaam is the subject of b.: therefore the
reference of a. is to Balak, out of whose hand Grod delivered Israel.

which was commanded (b.); while in a., they had done that which was forbidden (a.).

while in a., we have no longer the words of the ambassador, but of the Lord, who is
mentioned in a. Thus, in a., we have the “rumour” of a.; while in b., we have the
embassage sent among the heathen.

We must now come to the third class of arrangements, which, as we have said, is
a combination of the other two.
(3) Alternate and Introverted combined.
In these cases the general arrangement may be alternate, while some of its
members may consist of an introversion; or the general arrangement may be an
introversion, while some of its members may consist of alternations.


Take an example from Matt. xiii. 54 —58. Here, and throughout the work, we
employ capital letters for the general arrangement, and small letters for the subsidiary
members of it: —
A 1 a | And when he was come into his own country,
I b 1 he taught them in their synagogue,
B 1 insomuch that they were astonished, and said,
C | Whence hath this man this wisdom, and these mighty works ?
D I c 1 Is not this the carpenter’s son ?
I d| is not his mother called Mary ?
D I c | and his brethren, James, and Joses, and Simon, and Judas?
1 d 1 And his sisters, are they not all with us ?
C | Whence then hath this man all these things ?
B 1 And they were offended in him,
A a 1 But Jesus said unto them, A prophet is not without honour, save in his own
I country, and in his own house.
h 1 And he did not many mighty works there because of their unbelief.
(Matt. xiii. 54 —58.)
Here, while the arrangement as a whole is an Introversion, the members A. and A.,
D. and D., consist each of two members, which are strictly alternate. This produces
the combined result. In D., we have the father and mother of Jesus ; in D., his brethren
and sisters : that is, his father and brethren (males) in c. and c.; his mother and sisters
(females) in d. and d.
In C. and (7., we have the corresponding enquiries.
In B. and ß., the effect of his teaching; that is, in 8., astonishment, and in 8.,
offence.
In A and A., the extreme members, the correspondence is twofold, as it is in the
central members, D. and D.: a. answering to a., and b. to b. With regard to a. and a. ;
a., relates to our Saviour’s coming “into his own country”; a., to the treatment he
there met with. With regard to b. and b ., these refer to two things that generally
went together in our Lord’s ministrations, namely, in b., His teaching , and in 1., the
working of miracles.
These examples will suffice to illustrate the great principles which are found to
prevail in the structure of the Holy Scriptures. Several of the Epistles have been
completely set forth in Greek and English by Thomas Boys in his Tactica Sacra,
published in 1824, viz., the two Epistles to the Thessalonians, the second Epistle of
St. Peter, and the Epistle to Philemon. By a careful study of the marks made in his
Greek Testament, the Epistle to the Hebrews has recently been completed, and the
whole of these we hope to publish if we receive sufficient encouragement to enable us to
do so. Ho greater proof of Inspiration could be furnished than the most elaborate
and complicated, yet perfect, structure of the second Epistle of St. Peter. As we gaze
upon it, it seems impossible to imagine that all its details were fully present to the
mind of that human instrument who was perceived to be “ unlearned and ignorant ”
(Acts iv. 13).
Enough has been said to show the object of the present work. In 1825 Thomas
Boys published his Key to the Book of Psalms. It contained only sixteen examples,
with a full dissertation on the principles involved, and many illustrations, some of
which, with his remarks, we have given above. He lived to complete nearly the
whole of the Psalms, but it is not until now that they have been published. We
believe that this is the first time that such a work has been laid before the public:


for, writing in 1886, in a preface to The Paragraph Psalter , Dr. Westcott, tlie present
Bishop of Durham, recorded his testimony —“As far as I am aware, no attempt has
been made to exhibit the general structure of the Psalms.” This refers, of course, to
their being musically treated. But a most cursory reference to that work will show
the immense difference between the results respectively obtained.
It will be noticed that in the arrangement of the Psalms, some of the members are
long, and consist of a number of verses. We believe that in each such case there is a
perfect plan and structure, if we could only discover it. In many cases we have done
this; but in a supplementary manner, so as not to interfere with the more concise view
which the shorter and more condensed arrangement affords ; we have generally
repeated the words of the member, that its plan may be exhibited to the eye. It may
be taken for granted that all the longer members of the correspondences admit of
subdivision, and form complete arrangements of their own.
It may be that the correspondence is not so clearly marked in some cases as it is
in others ; and that the corresponding members may not seem exactly to resemble
each other in every point; but still, a correspondence is evident, either in their lead-
ing topics, in their relative situations, or in their leading or final terms. In a Scrip-
ture where the structure is alternate,
A 1
B|
*\
A. and A., may be two prayers; while B. and B , may be two thanksgivings. Or A. and
A., may be two exhortations , while B. and 8., may be two reasons or inducements.
Or A. and A , may be two addresses to the Almighty ; while B. and 8., may be, not
addresses, but merely descriptions of His attributes, operations or judgments. Thus,
though in each of these cases (A. and A., and B. and 8 ., respectively) there may not be
an exact resemblance in every particular, yet there is a perfect and essential correspon-
dence. They correspond in their relative situations, and on further examination they
will probably be found to correspond also in their leading terms. That is to say,
A. and A , will be found to begin or end with similar words or phrases, and so too
with B. and B.
Such is the character of the correspondences and resemblances which are now to
be exhibited in the Psalms. The advantage of knowing and observing them is indis-
putable and incalculable. They will tell us what was the particular plan of the com-
position ; what is the object and purport; where the various topics begin, and where
they terminate, and at what point they are resumed. For this reason the present
work may be correctly called “A Key to the Psalms.” It will be observed that the
arrangements are such as exist in the Psalms; they are not plans made for them, but
are those of the sacred writers; not that finality is claimed in each particular case.
There are evident traces in the Author’s manuscript that he was not, himself, always
perfectly satisfied. In one or two cases the word “ doubtful ” is written in pencil,
either by his own or some other hand. Some were evidently left for further
consideration, while a few were left incomplete, and one (Ps. cxix.) was not touched,
beyond a few pencil-marks in his Bible.
In all cases, what the Author is responsible for, and what the Editor is responsible


for, is clearly indicated. All editorial additions are marked (Ed.), or are put in square
brackets, thus, [...].
One liberty has, however, been taken. We have removed all emendations from
the text, giving them in notes where necessary or important.
This brings us to the question of the text, which might be thought an indifferent
matter. We decided to give the Authorised Version ; but from what Bible should we
take it ? In which edition should we find it ? It is not generally known that there
is no edition in current use that exactly represents that of 1611. The spelling has
been entirely remodelled ; many changes have been introduced, no one can tell when,
or by whom, or by what authority! Some are evidently cases of intentional correction,
others are clearly from negligent oversight. Not to incur undue responsibility
ourselves, we decided to adopt the current text of the Authorised Version, as it is
printed by the Revisers in The Parallel Bible of 1885. But we have indicated in the
notes where this differs verbally from the original edition of 1611. The only case in
which we have varied from this is in the type employed in printing the Divine Names
and Titles.
It is well known that though our Triune God is one Lord, yet many names and
titles are used to set forth the various relationships which he sustains to the creatures
of His hand. Just as with ourselves: a person has a name by which he is known to and
addressed by strangers , another by which he is known to his friends , another by which
he is known to relatives , and yet another used only in the bosom of his family , unknown,
it may be, by all beside.
So with the Divine Being: there are many names, each of which bespeaks some
peculiar relationship. These, though common to the whole Bible, abound in the
Psalms. But the Authorised Version does not preserve them, either by uniformity of
type or consistency of translation. Take, for example, the following passages from the
Psalms: —
“As for the El, his way is perfect: the word of Jehovah is tried : he is a buckler to all those
that trust in him. For who is Eloha save Jehovah ?or who is a rock save our Elohim ? It is
the El that girdeth me with strength.” (Ps. xviii. 30—32.)
“Jehovah liveth; and blessed be my rock; and let the Elohim of my salvation be exalted.
It is the El that avengeth me, and subdueth the people under me.” (Ps. xviii. 46, 47.)

going down thereof.” (Ps. 1,1.)

name, Jah, and rejoice before him.” (Ps. Ixviii. 4.)

These examples will be sufficient to show the importance of preserving these
Divine names. This we have endeavoured to do, not by altering the translation of the
Authorised Version, but by merely changing the types. To keep this change within
the smallest possible limits, we have preserved the plan observed in the Authorised
Version, where “Lord” is already used for Jehovah, and “God” for Elohim,
printing the names as follows :
1. El = God.

9- mco, Messiah is translated “ the Anointed,” which words we have printed in capital
letters.
10. Elyon is generally, but not always, translated “ Most High.” This we have
always printed in capital letters and indicated the other translations in the notes.
11. Shaddai is translated, the Almighty. It is so printed, and pointed out in the notes.
The types, therefore, will always tell the reader exactly what the Divine Name is
in the Hebrew: and, if he bears in mind the following explanations, he need never be at
a loss for the key to this important subject.
1. EL God. In arriving at true knowledge concerning any word or expres-
sion in the Hebrew Scriptures it is necessary to note its first occurrence, as it is
generally important in helping us to fix on its essential meaning. El is probably a
primitive word, but so adapted as to form a participle of the verb bw and b'w, to he
strong, powerful, mighty. Hence it is always associated with the Deity as the source of
all power and might. Its first occurrence is in Gen. xiv. 18—20, in combination with
{■•l% (see No. 10), i.e., El-Elyon, El Most-High. The source of the Patriarch’s
str ngth was the Mighty God (Is. x. 21). And that Blessed One, when he became
“ a worm and no man,” and was humbled to “ the dust of death,” cried to the same
Mighty God as the source of His strength —Ps. xxii. x. —El-i, El-i, “My God, my
God.” (Matt, xxvii. 46.)
2. ELOHA (niV«), GOD. This is the singular form of Elohim (see No. 3). It
denotes abstract Deity, the one living and true God, who alone is worthy of
adoration. The name is always used with reference to “ the eternal power and
Godhead” as opposed to all false gods; and with reference to His power and
beneficence as opposed to the workings and characteristics of demons. It occurs
*57 times: the first of which is in Deut. xxxii. 15
kicked . . . then he forsook Eloha which made him, and lightly esteemed the Rock
of his salvation. They provoked him to jealousy with strange gods, with abomina-
tions provoked they him to anger. They sacrificed unto devils, not to Eloha ; to


Elollim ‘whom they knew not, to new gods that came newly up, whom your fathers
feared not. Of the Rock that begat thee thou art unmindful, and hast forgotten
El that formed thee.” Here, Eloha is the one true God, alone to be worshipped,
while El is the mighty Creator.
Neh. ix. 17, jB, is a beautiful example of His beneficent acts: “Thou art
Eloha ready to pardon, gracious and merciful, slow to anger, and of great kindness,
and forsookest them not. Yea, when they had made them a molten calf, and said, This
is thy Elollim.” Eloha therefore is the Adorable One.
3. ELOHIM God. This is the plural of “Eloha,” and occurs 2,700
times. It is from the same root as “El” and “Eloha,” and conveys the same idea of
power and might , but it is distinguished from them in its use by the Holy Spirit. El
denotes the source of all power ; Eloha denotes the eternal power and Godhead as the
alone object of worship ; while Elohlm is the displayer of that power in creation.
Gen. i. 1 (its first occurrence) teaches us this: “In the beginning Elollim created the
heaven and the earth.”
Our relationship to these titles is thus clearly defined. El is the source of our
power, the Mighty God; Eloha is the object of our worship, the Adorable One;
Elohlm is our Creator. Of course, each is true of all the three titles, inasmuch as
each name belongs and refers to one and the same Person; but each defines our
peculiar relationship to Him. Elohlm is related to me only as a Creator. Whenever
this title, therefore, is used of the Divine Being, it implies the God of power and
creation, and marks our relation to Him as such.
4 Jehovah (nirr), Lord. Prom its possible derivation it denotes the self-
existing One; He who was, and is, and ever will be; He who has continuous and
absolute existence. Whenever it is used it implies the covenant relation which He
sustains to the works which He has created. We can say “my God” (or my Elohim),
but not my Jehovah, because Jehovah is my Elohim. We can say, “the God (or the
Elohim) of Israel,” but not the Jehovah of Israel, for Jehovah is the covenant God
of Israel.
Jehovah is the “ I am,” and this name tells of the eternal unchanging presence of
a covenant God. (See John viii. 58; Ex. xxxiv. 6,7, 10.)
He appeared to the Patriarchs by the name of El Shaddai* (see below No. 11),
Ex. vi. 2. He was not knownf by the name Jehovah to them.
Its first occurrence is in Gen. ii. 4; (c/. iii. 22), in combination with Elollim
(see No. 3), and probably the compound (and all the compounds have their own
peculiar signification) may have special reference to Eden; as Jehovah has to the
Exodus; and Jehovah-Elohim-Shaddai (the Lord God Almighty) to the restored nation.
Jehovah, then, defines our covenant relationship to the Divine Being, as distinct
from a mere creature relationship. See the beautiful illustrations in 2 Chron. xviii. 31;
xix. 6, 7 ; and compare Psalms xiv. and liii.
5- JAH (rr), LORD, is composed of the first two letters of the word Jehovah,
* viz,, Gen. xvii. I; xxxv. 11 ; and xlviii. 3.
f means to perceive, to understand. Not merely to know of, or know about, but to discern
and know fully and experimentally. Thus tbe patriarchs did not know Him. They knew of His
name, but they were not aware of all that it meant and involved for them.


and is possibly an abbreviation of it, containing the essence of its meaning in a
concentrated form. Its first occurrence is Ex. xv. 2, in the first Song, a Song of
Redemption; and seems to be thus specially associated with Redemption -work in
all its fulness as to origin and design. It occurs forty-nine times {i.e., seven times
seven), forty-three of which are in the Psalms. The other six are Ex. xv. 2; xvii. 16;
Is. xii. 2 ; xxvi. 4 ; xxxviii. 11 (twice). All these may be studied with special reference to
its distinctive use. One who can use it, is one who can call the Divine Being, not merely
Jehovah {i.e., my God), but my covenant God who has Redeemed me.
6. ADON (p"»«), lord. This is always printed “ Lord” in the A.V. To distinguish
it from No. Bwe have printed it, in the Key, without the capital letter. It is from the
unused root jin, to he low, humble, or inferior (hence, ps a foundation,base,pedestal,threshold).
Then transitively, to make low or humble; to determine, dispose, rule. Hence Adon means
ruler, master, determiner, commander. Its first occurrence fixes its meaning, Gen. xviii. 12,
where Sarah calls Abraham her Adon (1 Pet. iii. 6). The word, therefore, expresses our
acknowledged deferential position in relation to the Deity, Adon is the common word
for superiority of position, whether as wife to husband, master to servant, or king to
subject, &c., both Divine and Human. When used of the Divine Being it has the
article Ha-AdOH.
Its use will be easily seen from the following passages :
In Ex. xxiii. 17; xxxiv. 23, it is combined with Jehovah, “Thy males shall
appear before the Adon Jehovah”* {i.e., the Covenant God, who is also thy Ruler
and therefore issues this command).
Josh. iii. 11, 13 ; and Zech. vi. 3, we read of “the Adon of all the earth.”
Ps. xcvii. 5 ; Mic. iv. 13 ; Zech. iv. 14: “ the Adon of the whole earth.”
Ps. cx. 1: “ Jehovah said unto ray Adon.” (See Matt. xxii. 43, 44.)
Ps. cxiv. 7 : “ Tremble thou earth at the presence of Adon, at the presence of the
Eloha of Jacob” (that is, tremble thou earth at the presence of thy Ruler, at the
presence of the Adorable One of Jacob).
In Is. i. 24; iii. 1; x. 16, 33 ; xix. 4; Mai. iii. 1: “ Thus saith the Adon, Jehovah
of Hosts.”t
These are all the occurrences; and they relate specially to Jesus, who was made low,
and “ humbled Himself. J” and on this very account was “ made (Ha-Adon) lord.” See
Acts ii. 36 and Phil. ii. 10, 11. Jehovah He could not be “ made ”; that He ever was
and ever will be. But because of His wondrous humiliation all power was given unto
Him in heaven and in earth, and He will presently exercise that power on earth as in
heaven, as so many of the Psalms testify.
7. ADONIM (o*Hn)> L-o-r-d, is the plural of “Adon.” Its first occurrence is
Gen. xix. 2, 18. It is the plural of majesty and excellence, and carries with it all that
* This combination is always read by the Jews as “ Lord God.”
t The word Host, or Hosts, is used of armies, angels, stars, &c. See Ps. xxxiii. 6 ;
xliv. 9; and Ixviii. il, “ the company (of women).” The first occurrence of the verb is Ex. xxxyiii. 8,
where it is used twice in connection with women. Compare i Sam. ii. 22.


relates to “Adon,” only in a greater and higher degree. Perhaps ownership may he
more strongly implied. All the instances occur in the Psalms except the following:—
Deut. x. 17. “For Jehovah your Elohim, is Elohim of Elohim, and Adonim of
Adonim, the great El, the mighty, and the terrible, which regardeth
not persons, nor taketh reward,”
Neh. hi. 5. “ The work of their Adonim.”
viii. 10. “Holy unto our Adonim.”
x. 29. “Jehovah our Adonim.”
Is. li. 22. “ Thus saith thy Adonim, Jehovah ; and thy Elohim.”
B. ADHNAI Opm), Lord. This title (unlike Adon and Adonim) is used exclusively of
the Divine Being. There are grave differences of opinion among grammarians as to the
precise nature and form of the worH. There can be little doubt, however, that when
the sacred, ineffable word “ Jehovah” came to be regarded as so sacred that it was
considered sacrilege to pronounce it according to its original and proper pronunciation,
its vowel points were used with the word “Adon” with the possessive pronoun,
and thus Adonee (*?'w), my Lord, became Adonai (w). This is the reason why the
manuscripts of the Hebrew Bible so frequently have Adonai, where the printed text
has Jehovah, and vice versa. Adonai occurs about 430 times; and the Massorah gives
a list of 134 places where it stands alone, instead of the word Jehovah.
Wherever, therefore, we have this word Adonai by itself, we are to understand it
as though it were read Jehovah.
These three words may therefore be thus distinguished:— Adon is the lord in
power and rule. AdOllilll is the L-o-x’-d, as owner and proprietor. While Adonai is
Jehovah, the Lord in the relationship of covenant blessing.
9. MESSIAH (mpo), The Anointed. This title of Jesus is generally thus
translated. It is from miia, to anoint , dedicate. The Greek is X/Hcrrds, because He is
the Saviour, anointed, and appointed, by the Father. Ps. ii. 2 is directly referred to
the Saviour in Acts iv. 26, &c.
10. ELYON (ply), The Most High. This name is from rf», to he high, lofty.
Elyoil is the High and Lofty One inhabiting eternity. “ God over all, blessed for ever.”
“ The Highest.” It occurs 33 times, 22 of which are in the Psalms.* It sets forth
the sublimity of the Deity as high above all; as Elohim does His creation relation-
ship; Jehovah, His covenant relationship; and Shaddai, His bounty and His all-fulness
of blessing for man.
11. Shaddai ('■nr), The Almighty. It is from the unused root rrrr, to moisten ,
bedew, give drink to, to suckle. Hence -nr, the breast of a woman. Shaddai, therefore,,
denotes not so much the power or might of the Deity, as the fulness of His resources,
His richness and bounty. Shaddai is the All-bountiful One. The first occux’rence of
this name (Gen. xvii. 1) gives us its meaning. For fifteen years there had been no
appearance of God to Abram, and no Divine communication. Abram had obtained a
son after his own will, and our attention is drawn to the Divine displeasure by the
silence of Heavexx in Gen. xvi. 16, and xvii. 1. Whexx God at length appeared, He
revealed Himself by a new title, “El Shaddai,” the All-bountiful One, intimating that
supply was to be sought—even the supply in fulfilment of the Divine promise —< nly
in Divinely -sanctioned ways, and not by human devices. Gen. xvii. 1, “I am
* The English “ Most High” in Ps. lyi. 2, and xcii. 8, is another word, ai'n ( marom ), lifted up,.


El Shaddai, walk before me,” &c, When God was calling Abraham to walk before
Him in the path of faith and dependence, the name which he used of Himself told
Abraham of the relationship which He would sustain toward him, as one Almighty
in resources to support and defend him, and to supply all his need. So, likewise, when
we are commanded (2 Cor. vi. 14 —18) to come out from evil associations and walk
before God, as Abraham did, and to be separate, the title chosen is that which was
used with Abraham, and for the same reason, “ Ye shall be my sons and daughters,
saith the Lord Almighty.”
See also Gen. xxviii. 3; xxxv. 11 ; xliii. 14; xlviii. 3; xlix. 25; Ex. yi. 3 ; and
Ezek. x. 3. These are all the places where Shaddai occurs in combination with El.
It occurs without El in Hum. xxiy. 4, 16; Ruth i. 20, 21; Ps. Ixviii. 14; xci. 1
Three times in the prophets —Is. xiii. 6; Ezek. i. 24; Joel i. 13; and 31 times in Job.
We lose the force of Ruth i. 20, 21, by not rendering thus : “ The All-bountifal hath
dealt very bitterly with me”; “The All-bountiful hath afflicted me.” So also
Joel i. 13: “It shall come as a destruction ( tiy) from the All-bountiful (’jc)”
Compare “the wrath of the Lamb” (Rev. vi. 16, 17).
Shadilai is not only invariably translated the Almighty, but the Almighty is
never used as the translation of any other Hebrew word.
The Titles of the Psalms form too large a subject to enter upon here, but a care-
ful study of the Appendix will show one of the uses of the Titles, especially the names
of the Sacred Penmen, in helping to determine the correspondences of the groups in
the different Books.
It is not the object of this work to furnish a commentary, or even exegetical notes.
It will lay the foundation for new studies and new works, and thus prove the truth of
the Saviour’s words: —“ Herein is that saying true . . . other men laboured, and ye
are entered into their labours.” (John iv. 38.)
E. W. BELLINGER.
Bremgarten,
Woking.

Note. —ln Psalm iii. we meet for the first time the word “Selah.” It seems impossible to
speak positively as to its meaning. As to its use, it is often found at the end of a member, and is
thus connected with the structure. It seems to connect, by a pause, two thoughts, the former
receiving additional emphasis in the latter, especially where the Psalm ends with “ Selah.” See
Psalm xxiv. 4, and xxv. I: also Ivii. 3, &c. “ Selah ” occurs 71 times in the Psalms :17 times in
Book i, 30 times in Book ii., 20 times in Book iii., and 4 times in Book v.
In sixteen of the Psalms it occurs once (vii., xx., xxi., xliv., xlvii., xlviii., 1., liv., lx., Ixi., Ixxv.,
Ixxxi., Ixxxii., Ixxxiii., Ixxxv., cxliii.). In fifteen Psalms it occurs twice (iv., ix., xxiv., xxxix.,
xlix., lii., Iv., Ivii., lix., Ixii., Ixvii., Ixxvi., Ixxxiv., Ixxxvii., Ixxxviii.). In seven Psalms it
occurs three times (iii,, xxxii,, xlvi., Ixvi., Ixviii., Ixxvii., cxl.), and in one Psalm it occurs four
times , viz., Ps. Ixxxix. But never in a Psalm without a Title.
In Hah. iii. it also occurs three times, bringing up the total number of its occurrences to 74
times in the Old Testament.— (Ed.)
* A and A refer to Jehovah, while B and B refer to “ the sons of men.”

Psalms ix. and x. are linked together by an irregular alphabet running through the two
acrosticallv, as indicated (Psalm x. beginning with “ Lamed” (b), which is the beginning of the last
half of the alphabet); also by x. having no title ; by Psalm ix. ending with “ Selah,” implying a
pause between that and another portion, and by the repetition of similar w T ords and phrases. The
“Koph” (p), in ix, 19, instead of the repeated “ Kaph ” (3) of v. 18, calls our attention to its
repetition in x. 12, which begins with the same word and with the same prayer.-— (Ed.)

Itealm xvn.
A Prayer of David.
A i Hear the right, 0 Lord, attend unto my cry, give ear unto my prayer, that goeth
not out of feigned lips. 2 Let my sentence come forth from thy presence; let
thine eyes behold the things that are equal. 3 Thou hast proved mine heart;
thou hast visited me in the night; thou hast tried me, and shalt find nothing; I
am purposed that my mouth shall not transgress. 4 Concerning the works of
men, by the word of thy lips I have kept me from the paths of the destroyer.
5 Hold up my goings in thy paths, that my footsteps slip not. 6 I have called
upon thee, for thou wilt hear me, 0 God: incline thine ear unto me, and hear my
speech.
B a 7 Shew thy marvellous loving-kindness, 0 thou that savest by thy right
hand them which put their trust in thee from those that rise up ngainst them.
8 Keep me as the apple of the eye, hide me under the shadow of thy wings,
b 9 From the wicked, that oppress me, from my deadly enemies, who com-
pass me about.
c 10 They are inclosed in their own fat: with their mouth they speak
proudly. 11 They have now compassed us in our steps : they have set
their eyes bowing down to the earth;
C | 12 Like as a lion that is greedy of his prey,
C | and as it were a young lion lurking in secret places.
B a | 13 Arise, 0 Lord, disappoint him, cast him down: deliver my soul
h from the wicked which is thy sword: 14 From men which are thy hand,
O Lord, from men of the world,
c which have 1 their portion in this life, and whose 2 belly thou fillest with
thy hid treasure: they are full of children, and leave the rest of their
substance to their babes.
A 15 As for me, I will behold thy face in righteousness: I shall be satisfied, when I
awake, with thy likeness.

Itealm xviii.
A a | 1 I will love thee, O Lord, my strength.
b j 2 The Lord is my rock, and my fortress, and my deliverer; my God, my strength, in whom
( I will trust; my buckler, and the horn of my salvation, and my high tower.
B 3 I will call upon the Lord, who is worthy to be praised : so shall I be saved from mine
enemies. The sorrows of death compassed me, and the floods of ungodly men made me
afraid. 5 The sorrows of hell compassed me about: the snares of death prevented me. 6 In
my distress I called upon the Lord, and cried unto my God: he heard my voice out of his
temple, and my cry came before him, even into his ears. 7 Then the earth shook and trembled;
the foundations also of the hills moved and were shaken, because he was wroth. 8 There
went up a smoke out of his nostrils, and fire out of his mouth devoured : coals were kindled
by it. 9 He bowed the heavens also, and came down: and darkness was under his feet.
10 And he rode upon a cherub, and did fly; yea, he did fly upon the wings of the wind.
11 He made darkness his secret place; his pavilion round about him were dark waters and
thick clouds of the skies. 12 At the brightness that ions before him his thick clouds passed,
hail stones and coals of fire. 13 The Lord also thundered in the heavens, and the Highest
gave his voice; hail stones and coals of fire. 14 Yea, he sent out his arrows, and scattered
them ; and he shot out lightnings, and discomfited them. 15 Then the channels of waters were
seen, and the foundations of the world were discovered at thy rebuke, O Lord, at the blast of
the breath of thy nostrils. 16 He sent from above, he took me, he drew me out of many
waters. 17 He delivered me from my strong enemy, and from them which hated me : for they
were too strong for me. 18 They prevented me in the day of my calamity : but the Lord was
my stay. 19 He brought me forth also into a large place; he delivered me, because he
delighted in me.
C 20 The Lord rewarded me according to my righteousness; according to the cleanness of
my hands hath he recompensed me. 21 For I have kept the ways of the Lord, and have not
wickedly departed from my God. 22 For all his judgments were before me, and I did not
put away his statutes from me. 23 I was also upright before him, and I kept myself from
mine iniquity. 24 Therefore hath the Lord recompensed me according to my righteousness,
according to the cleanness of my hands in his eyesight.
G 25 With the merciful thou wilt shew thyself merciful; with an upright man thou wilt shew
thyself upright; 26 With the pure thou wilt shew thyself pure; and with the froward thou
wilt shew thyself froward. 27 For thou wilt save the afflicted people; but wilt bring down
high looks.
B 28 For thou wilt light my candle : the Lord my God will enlighten my darkness. 29 For by
thee I have run through a troop; and by my God have I leaped over a wall. 30 As for God, his
way is perfect : the word of the Lord is tried : he is a buckler to all those that trust in him,
31 For who is GOD save the Lord ? or who is a rock save our God ? 32 It is God that girdeth
me with strength, and maketh my way perfect. 33 He maketh my feet like hinds’ feet, and
setteth me upon my high places. 34 He teacheth mj r hands to war, so that a bow of steel is
broken by mine arms. 35 Thou hast also given me the shield of thy salvation: and thy right
hand hath holden me up, and thy gentleness hath made me great. 36 Thou hast enlarged my
steps under me, that my feet did not slip. 37 I have pursued mine enemies, and overtaken
them : neither did I turn again till they were consumed. 38 I have wounded them that they
were not able to rise : they are fallen under my feet. 39 For thou hast girded me with
strength unto the battle : thou hast subdued under me those that rose up against me. 40 Thou
hast also given me the necks of mine enemies; that I might destroy them that hate me.
41 They cried, but there was none to save them ; even unto the Lord, but he answered them
not. 42 Then did I beat them small as the dust before the wind : I did cast them out as the
dirt in the streets. 43 Thou hast delivered me from the strivings of the people; and thou hast
made me the head of the heathen : a people whom I have not known shall serve me. 44 As
soon as they hear of me, they shall obey me ; the strangers shall submit themselves unto me.
45 The strangers shall fade away, and be afraid out of their close places. 46 The Lord liveth ;
and blessed he my rock; and let the God of my salvation be exalted. 47 It is God that
avengeth me, and subdueth the people under me. 48 He delivereth me from mine enemies :
yea, thou liftest me up above those that rise up against me : thou hast delivered me from the
violent man.
A aI 49 Therefore will I give thanks unto thee, 0 Lord, among the heathen, and sing praises unto
I thy name.
bI 50 Great deliverance giveth he to his king; and sheweth mercy to his Anointed, to
I David, and to his seed for evermore.

A I T l ie heavells declare the glory of God ; and the firmament sheweth his

knowledge. 3 There is no speech nor language, where their voice is not heard.

world.
B In them 1 hath he set a tabernacle for the sun, 5 Which is as a bridegroom
coming out of his chamber, and rejoiceth as a strong man to run a race. 6 His
going forth is from the end of the heaven, and his circuit unto the ends of it •
and there is nothing hid from the heat thereof.
A 7 The law of the Lord is perfect, converting the soul: the testimony of the Lord is
sure making wise the simple. 8 The statutes of the Lord are right, rejoicing the
fii : -r e Co , mmari dHient of the Lord is pure, enlightening the eyes. 9 The fear
ot the Lord is clean, enduring for ever : the judgments of the Lord are true and
righteous altogether. 10 More to be desired are they than gold, yea, than much

B 11 Moreover by them 1 is thy servant warned: and in keeping of them there is

Psalm XlX.— The Skeleton.
A | I—4-.1 —4-. The heavens.
B | -4 —6. The sun in them (dui).
A | 7 —lo. The scriptures.
B | 11 —14. Thy servant in them (Dim).
1 cm, in them. The same word in v. 4 and v. 11.
A wonderful truth is brought out by a pencilled jotting in the author’s Hebrew Bible, to the
effect that many of the words in A and B are astronomical terms, and refer to the Sun and the
Heavens, which are the subject of A and B. Thus in B, inw “ warned ',” v. 1 1, is from vn, to be
bright, to shine, make light: then, to teach, to admonish. DiaiTD, “keeping,” v. 11, is from
to he stiff, rigid , as a fence, to keep, observe, watch , as the heavens, or for the morning, Ps. exxx. 6 ;
Is. xxi. 11: also as the heavenly bodies observe God’s ordinances. PilM'Jtlr, “errors,” r. 12, is from'
to wander, as the planets do. 31m, “ keep back ”v. 13, to hold back, restrain. “ hare
dominion over” v. 13, from hwo, to rule. Spoken of the sun and moon in Gen, i. 18, “ The sun to
rule the day, &c.,” Ps. cxxxvi. 8, 9. Also in A, ni'iro, “converting ,” v. 7is from iw, to turn,
be turned, return, as the sun in the heavens. reny, “ testimony,” v. 7, is from 1131, to turn back,
return , to go over again, repeat, reiterate, say again and again; hence, a witness; spoken of the
sun in Ps. Ixxxix. 37 as “the faithful witness in heaven.” iiJDNJ, “sure,” v. 7, is from (on, to
stay, be lasting , endure; “faithful,” ns the sun, the witness in heaven, Ps. Ixxxix. 37. j-iTmd,
“ enlightening,” v. 8, is from Tim, to be or become light, to shine, be bright, to give light as the sun,
Gen. i. 15, 17, 18 ; Is. lx. 19; Ezek. xxxii. 7. Observe also that in A, v. 4, is used by the Holy
Spirit, of the gospel in Rom. x. 18, thus uniting the two subjects and linking them together.— (Ed.)
The term “ Scriptures,” as representing and describing A, is weak, and fails to convey the
fulness of the combined Law, plus Testimony, plus Statutes, plus Commandment, plus Godly fear,
plus Judgments. The soul, in all this, is as the sun in the heavens! How small a key (Dim, verses
4 and 11) to open such a magnificent arrangement.— (Ed.)

1 Margin, “ turn to ashes." The Lord accepts a sacrifice by consuming it with fire from
heaven: the fire falling on the substitute instead of upon the offerer. So the Lord accepted
Abel’s offering, Gen. iv. 4, “ had respect ”to it, and Heb. xi. 4, “ testifying of his gifts.” The fire
on the altar was from heaven, Lev. ix. 24, and when sacrifices were accepted away from this fire
on the altar, the fire of the Lord fell specially from heaven. See Jud. vi. 21, 1 Kings xviii. 38,
1 Chron. xxi. 26, and 2 Chron. vii. I. —(Ed.)

|)salm xxiii.
A Psalm of David.
A 1 The Lord is my shepherd; I shall not want. 2 He maketh me to lie down in
green pastures : he leadeth me beside the still waters. 3 He restoreth my soul;
he leadeth me in the paths of righteousness for his name’s sake.
B 4 Yea though I walk through the valley of the shadow of death, I will fear no
evil: tor thou art with me; thy rod and thy staff they comfort me.
B 5 Thou prepares! a table before me in the presence of mine enemies: thou
anointest my head with oil; my cup runneth over.
A 6 Surely goodness and mercy shall follow me all the days of my life: and I will
dwell in the liouse of the Lord for ever.
Psalm XXIII. —The Skeleton,
A | I —3. Third person, “ He.” )
B| 4. Second person, “ Thou” ) biiee P*
B| 5. Second person, “ Thou.” ) „
A| 6. Third person, “He.” ( Servant.
Note.— A and A speak only of supply, while B and B speak of danger. There is also an
alternation in positives and negatives. Further, in each of the four members there are two leading
nouns:— °
I | Food and drink (“pastures ” and “ waters”).
2 | Bod and staff.
3 1 Food and drink (“ table ” and “ cup ”).
4 | Goodness and mercy.
Again, in— A | The Lord is spoken of.
B | The Lord is spoken to.
B ) The Lord is spoken to.
A | The Lord is spoken of.—[S.T.]
The Editor appends a further note, calling attention to the fact that, as the shepherd does
everythin;/ tor his sheep, so Jehovah is engaged in all His attributes and titles as “ the great
{Shepherd” of His people. Hence:—
In v. I we have Jehovah Jieeh (Gen. xxii. 14), “The Lord will provide:” that is whv
“ 1 shall not want.” J
In v. 2 we have Jehovah Shalom (Judges vi. 24), “ The Lord send peace,” leading beside
the waters of quietness, rest or comfort (v. 4).
In v. 3 we have Jehovah Rophi (Ex. xv. 26), “ The Lord that healeth thee ” for “He
restoreth (or reviveth) my soul (or life).”
In v. 3 we have Jehovah Tsidkentj (Jer. xxiii. 6, xxxiii. 16), “ The Lord our righteousness ”
guiding me in “ paths of righteousness.”
In v. 4 we have Jehovah Shammah (Ez. xlviii. 35),“ The Lord is there :” for in “ the valley
of the shadow of death . . . Thou art with me.” J
In v. 5 w r e have Jehovah Nissi (Ex. xvii. 15), “The Lord my banner,” and Jehovah
Sabaioth (i Sam. i, 3, &c.) “ The Lord of Hosts,” against all “ mine enemies.”
Inv. 5 w 7 ehave Jehovah Mekaddisdchem (Ex. xxxi. 13, &c.}, “The Lord that sanctiheth
you,” anointing my head with oil.
Note also that in Ps. xxii. we have “ the Good Shepherd ” (John x. 11) in death ;in Ps xxiii
we have “the Great Shepherd” (Heb. xiii. 20), in resurrection ; while in Ps. xxiv. we have “the
chief Shepherd” (1 Pet. v. 4), in ijlonj. —(Ed.)

B | 12 —14. Didactic.
A | 15 —22. Supplicatory.
This is an acrostic Psalm, but not perfect. Its anomalies excite attention, and'show that
everything is not sacrificed to the alphabet. The 21 letters (instead of 22) —three sevens —give
prominence to the central letter (b) and verse. The double n (v. i, 2), and the double (v. 18, 19,
same word Msn in each case), emphasises the looking up of the soul, and the looking down of God in
answer. The fact that Ps. xxiv. ends with “ Selah ” connects xxiv. 4 with these verses, xxv. 1,2,
and 18, 19. (See Note on Ps. iii.) The first and last verses are also made to stand apart, and
out of the regular sequence.— (Ed.)

A Psalm of David.
A i The Lord is my light and my salvation; whom shall I fear ? the Lord is the

now shall mine head be lifted up above mine enemies round about me: therefore
will I offer in his tabernacle sacrifices of joy; I will sing, yea, 1 will sing praises

witnesses are risen up against me, and such as breathe out cruelty.

of the living. 14 Wait on the Lord : be of good courage, and he shall strengthen
thine heart: wait, I say, on the Lord.

Psalm xxix. is the sequel to Psalm xxviii., being the fulfilment of the promise in xxviii. 7. It
further corresponds by contrasting the “voice” of David (xxviii. 6) and the “ Voice of the Lord »»
(xxix. 3—9); and by showing how the Lord is and will he the “strength” of His people. Compare

one to the other alternately.— (Ed.)

s)salm xxxi.
To the chief Musician. A Psalm of David.
A i In thee, O Lobd, do I put my trust; let me never be ashamed: deliver me in thy righteousness
2 Bow down thine ear to me; deliver me speedily; be thou my strong rock, for an house of
defence to save me. 3 For thou art my rock and my fortress; therefore for thy name’s sake lead
me, and guide me. 4 Pull me out of the net that they have laid privily for me : for thou art my
strength. 5 Into thine hand I commit my spirit: thou hast redeemed me, 0 Lord God of truth.
6 I have hated them that regard lying vanities: but I trust in the Lord.
B 7 I will be glad and rejoice in thy mercy: for thou hast considered my trouble; thou hast
known my soul in adversities; 8 And hast not shut me up into the hand of the enemy : thou
hast set my feet in a large room.
A 9 Have mercy upon me, 0 Lord, for lam in trouble ; mine eye is consumed with grief, yea, my
soul and my belly. 10 For my life is spent with grief, and my years with sighing :my strength
faileth because of mine iniquity, and my bones are consumed. 11 I was a reproach among°all
mine enemies, but especially among my neighbours, and a fear to mine acquaintance : they that
did see me without fled from me. 12 lam forgotten as a dead man out of mind: lam like a
broken vessel. 13 For 1 have heard the slander of many: fear was on every side: while they
took counsel together against me, they devised to take away my life. 14 But I trusted in thee,
O Lord : I said, Thou art my God. 15 My times are in thy hand : deliver me from the hand of
mine enemies, and from them that persecute me. 16 Make thy face to shine upon thy servant:
save me for thy mercies’ 1 sake. 17 Let me not be ashamed, 0 Lord ; for I have called upon thee:
let the wicked be ashamed, and let them be silent in the grave. 18 Let the lying lips be put to
silence; which speak grievous things proudly and contemptuously against the righteous. 2
Ji 19 Oh how great is thy goodness, which thou hast laid up for them that fear thee ; which thou
hast wrought for them that trust in thee before the sons of men! 20 Thou shalt hide them in
the secret of thy presence from the pride of man : thou shalt keep them secretly in a pavilion
from the strife of tongues. 21 Blessed he the Lord : for he hath shewed me his marvellous
kindness in a strong city. 22 For I said in my haste, lam cut off from before thine eyes :
nevertheless thou heardest the voice of my supplications when I cried unto thee. 23 O love
the Lord, all ye his saints : for the Lord preserveth the faithful, and plentifully rewardeth the
proud doer. 24 Be of good courage, and he shall strengthen your heart, all ye that hope in
the Lord.

xxxiii.
A Rejoice in the Lord, 0 ye righteous: for praise is comely for the upright.
2 Praise the Lord with harp: sing unto him with the psaltery and an instrument
of ten strings. 3 Sing unto him anew song; play skilfully with a loud noise.
B 4 For the word of the Lord is right; and all his works are done in truth. He
loveth righteousness and judgment: the earth is full of the goodness of the
Lord. 6 By the word of the Lord were the heavens made ; and all the host of
them by the breath of his mouth. 7 He gathereth the waters of the sea
together as an heap: he layeth up the depth in storehouses.
A 8 Let all the earth fear the Lord: let all the inhabitants of the world stand in
awe of him.
B| 9 For he spake, and it was done; he commanded, and it stood fast.
C 10 r I he Lord bringeth the counsel of the heathen to nought: he maketh the
devices of the people of none effect. 11 The counsel of the Lord standeth for
ever, the thoughts of his heart to all generations.
Di 2 Blessed is the nation whose Giod is the Lord ; and the people whom he hath
chosen for his own inheritance.
G 13 The Lord looketh from heaven ; he beholdeth all the sons of men. 14 From
the place of his habitation he looketh upon all the inhabitants of the earth.
15 He fashioneth their hearts alike ;he considereth all their works. 16 There is
no king saved by the multitude of an host: a mighty man is not delivered by
much strength. 17 An horse is a vain thing for safety: neither shall he deliver
any by his great strength.
D 18 Behold, the eye of the Lord is upon them that fear him, upon them that
hope in his mercy ; 19 To deliver their soul from death, and to keep them
alive in famine. 20 Our soul waiteth for the Lord : he is our help and our
shield. 21 For our heart shall rejoice in him, because we have trusted in his
holy name. 22 Let thy mercy, 0 Lord, be upon us, according as we hope in
thee.

Itealm xxxiv.
A Psalm of David, when he changed his behaviour before Abimelech; who drove him away, and he
departed.
A 1 (n) I will bless the Lord at all times: his praise shall continually he in my
mouth. 2 (n) My soul shall make her boast in the Lord : the humble shall hear
thereof, and be glad.
B ( 3 (j) 0 magnify the Lord with me, and let us exalt his name together.
A 4 ("1) I sought the Lord, and he heard me, and delivered me from all my fears.
5 (il) They looked unto him, and were lightened: and their faces were not
ashamed. 6 (t) This poor man cried, and the Lord heard him, and saved him out
of all his troubles. 7 (n) The angel of the Lord encampeth round about them
that fear him, and delivereth them.
B 8 (id) O taste and see that the Lord is good : blessed is the man that trusteth in
him. 9 (’) 0 fear the Lord, ye his saints: for there is no want to them that
fear him. 10 (D) The young lions do lack, and suffer hunger: but they that
seek the Lord shall not want any good thing. 11 (b) Come, ye children,
hearken unto me: I will teach you the fear of the Lord. 12 (a) What man
is he that desireth life, and loveth many days, that he may see good ?
13 (3) Keep thy tongue from evil, and thy lips from speaking guile. 14 (and) De-
part from evil, and do good; seek peace, and pursue it.
C 13 (y) The eyes of the Lord are upon the righteous, and his ears are open unto
their cry.
D 16 (9) The face of the Lord is against them that do evil, to cut off the remem-
brance of them from the earth.
Gl7 (v) The righteous cry, and the Lord heareth, and delivereth them out of all
their troubles. 18 (p) The Lord is nigh unto them that are of a broken heart;
and saveth such as be of a contrite spirit. 19 (n) Many are the afflictions of the
righteous: but the Lord delivereth him out of them all. 20 (w) He keepeth all
his bones: not one of them is broken.
D 21 (n) Evil shall slay the wicked : and they that hate the righteous shall be
desolate.
D 22 (q) The Lord redeemeth the soul of his servants : and none of them that trust
in him shall be desolate.

Psalm xxxv.
A Psalm of David.
A | i Plead my cause, 0 Lord, with them that strive with me:
A | fight against them that fight against me.
B | 2 Take hold of shield and buckler, and stand up for mine help.
B 1 3 Draw out also the spear, and stop the way against them that persecute me :
C | say unto my soul, I am thy salvation.
C 4 Let them be confounded and put to shame that seek after my soul: let them be turned
back and brought to confusion that devise my hurt. 5 Let them be as chaff before the
wind: and let the angel of the Lord chase them, 6 Let their way be dark and slippery;
and let the angel of the Lord persecute them. 7 For without cause have they hid for me
their net in a pit, which without cause they have digged for my soul. 8 Let destruction
come upon him at unawares ; and let his net that he hath hid catch himself: into that very
destruction let him fall. J
D 9 And my soul shall be joyful in the Lord : it shall rejoice in his salvation. 10 All my bones
shall say, Lord, who is like unto thee, which deliverest the poor from him that is too strong
for him, yea, the poor and the needy from him that spoileth him?
E 1 11 False witnesses did rise up ; they laid to my charge things that I knew not.
F | 12 They rewarded me evil for good to the spoiling of my soul.
F 13 But as for me, when they were sick, my clothing was sackcloth: I humbled my soul
with fasting ; and my prayer returned into mine own bosom. 14 I behaved myself as
though he had been my friend or brother: I bowed down heavily, as one that mourneth
for his mother. 15 But in mine adversity they rejoiced, and gathered themselves together ;
yea, the abjects gathered themselves together against me, and I knew it not; they did tear
me, and ceased not: 16 With hypocritical mockers in feasts, they gnashed upon me with
their teeth. 17 Lord, how long wilt thou look on ? rescue my soul from their destructions,
my darling from the lions. 18 I will give thee thanks in the great congregation: I will
praise thee among much people, ig Let not them that are mine enemies wrongfully
rejoice over me: neither let them wink with the eye that hate me without a cause.
E 20 For they speak not peace: but they devise deceitful matters against them that are quiet in
the land. 21 Yea, they opened their mouth wide against me, and said, Aha, aha, our eye
hath seen it. 22 This thou hast seen, 0 Lord : keep not silence : 0 Lord, be not far from me.
23 Stir up thyself, and awake to my judgment, even unto my cause, my God and my Lord.
24 Judge me, 0 Lord my God, according to thy righteousness ; and let them not rejoice over
me. 25 Let them not say in their hearts, Ah, so would we have it : let them not say, Wo
have swallowed him up. 26 Let them be ashamed and brought to confusion together that
rejoice at mine hurt: let them be clothed with shame and dishonour that magnify themselves
against me. 27 Let them shout for joy, and be glad, that favour my righteous cause : yea,
let them say continually, Let the Lord be magnified, which hath pleasure in the prosperity of
his servant. 28 And my tongue shall speak of thy righteousness and of thy praise all the
day long.
